Talking

I Have Another Hope

— Respectfully

The First Hope is to Eliminate the Civil War. The Second Hope is to Cooperate Fully.

A few days ago, there was a sudden rumor that there were changes in the troops in Guangdong and Guangxi. At first, I definitely did not recognize that this was a fact. It must be a rumor created by speculators in the exchange, or propaganda by someone who hoped for civil strife in our country. Unexpectedly, on the sixth of this month, the newspapers could not cover it up. The major newspapers wrote a lot about the Guangdong and Guangxi troops entering Hunan. After the Guangxi army was stationed in Yongzhou, two more divisions arrived in Chenzhou. Two planes from Guangdong landed in Hengyang and other bad news. I understood that this was true and could not help but break out in a cold sweat. Because I agreed with the purpose of the Guangdong and Guangxi troops sending troops, but I did not agree with their separate actions without working together with the Central Government. Moreover, not only could they not work together, but they started a civil war first, which I extremely opposed. Because the day when civil war begins in our country is the day when our country will be destroyed, which can be asserted.

Strong neighbors are watching us intently, hoping that something will happen to our country, and fearing that nothing will happen to our country. Therefore, they do not hesitate to fabricate various rumors, incite local troops, destroy the entire country, and reap the benefits of being a fisherman. The so-called ingenious plan of using Chinese to control Chinese has long been known to passers-by. If the southern authorities disregard everything, act entirely on impulse, first burn the beanstalk to cook the beans, and then forget the disaster of biting the navel, and regard the country as a lonely gamble, and treat compatriots as enemies, even infants may not be willing to take this unwise move. Fortunately, recently, thanks to the appeals from all parties, letters and telegrams flew back and forth, and the voices were hoarse, and the southern authorities had been understood. The newspaper reported the day before yesterday that Chen Jitang had ordered the troops in front to stop advancing and advised Li and Bai to order the Guangxi troops entering Hunan to stop proceeding and wait for the central government to resolve the matter. Yesterday, the newspaper reported that Guangdong and Guangxi had withdrawn their troops that had arrived in Yongzhou. This kind of news is extremely hoped for by the people of our country. In this situation of panic and dense war clouds, it suddenly disappeared in a few days, and the fierce atmosphere was completely eliminated. First, I cannot but admire President Chiang's magnanimity and order the central troops in front to move backward and not resist. Second, I cannot but admire the southern generals for stating the great righteousness and following the good advice. In a few days, they gradually withdrew the advancing troops. This attitude of both parties being loyal to the country, removing personal impulses, and being open and aboveboard is what I originally hoped for.

The second hope is that although this war has been eliminated, the opinions of both parties may not have been fully compromised. We further hope that the authorities of both parties will eliminate prejudice, be open and honest, and work together to relieve the national crisis. In the situation of sharing the same boat, we should be aware of the warning of the lips being gone and the teeth being cold. Brothers quarreling within the walls, defending against insults from outside, the teachings are clear and can be learned from. Now that the Second Plenary Session is about to open, we eagerly hope that the southern authorities will send senior officials to the meeting as soon as possible to discuss the matter at length and cooperate fully, so that the internal troubles have been eliminated and the external disasters can also be resolved. This is also what our people eagerly expect.

Night

— Liu Zhiming

When I was a child, I was most afraid of the night. As soon as it got dark, I didn't dare to leave my family even one step. However, the houses in the countryside were both wide and large. The area illuminated by a kerosene lamp was only a circle nearby, and the shadow of a person projected on the wall was particularly large. If that shadow accidentally transformed into a terrifying shape in my eyes, I would cover my eyes with my palms and shout loudly. My mother had no way to cure my timidity. She always wanted me to go to bed early every day, because she had many things to do under the lamp at night, and I often disturbed her and couldn't feel at ease.

Speaking of my timidity, there is also a reason. At one time, my brother often twisted my grandmother to tell stories. Those stories were all about terrible zombies and legends of tigers eating people. In fact, we were all afraid of hearing such stories, but that kind of unexpected plot aroused our interest more than the stupid son-in-law specially told by our cook. Therefore, in a contradictory state of being afraid to hear and liking to hear, I dragged my mother and my brother dragged the maid and listened to that kind of story. Later, my grandmother died, but the terrifying images still remained in my mind and refused to dissipate, and instead became thicker: seeing an enlarged shadow would make me think of it as the incarnation of a zombie; hearing a little sound would make me think of a tiger crawling on the window. So I was most afraid of the night.

My father ran a shop in the town and lived at home for at most three or four days a month. After my brother and I graduated from elementary school, because of our different personalities, my brother continued his studies, and I helped my father's shop. His intention was that we could receive the effects of both parties by being close to our personalities. But when my brother went to the county town to go to middle school, and I went to my father's shop in the town to help, there were only my mother and a maid at home. The customs in the countryside always felt that it was not very appropriate for a man to be missing from the home. At first, my father wanted to go home every day, but my mother thought that my father was old, busy with shop affairs during the day, and it would be too hard to run so many roads home at night. After discussion, I lived at home. Although the shops in the town closed very early, after eating dinner and settling the accounts, it was about to be the first watch. When the moonlight was good, I could go home by the moon. Usually, I carried a lantern from the shop and groped my way home in the dim light.

Walking at night was really a difficult hurdle for me. The terrifying images that had been lurking since I was a child seemed to be active around me at that time. When the moonlight was good, looking at the black shadow in front of me, I would imagine that there was a strong man lying in ambush there (because my knowledge told me that there would be no zombies and tigers). He would suddenly jump out like a hound when I got closer. As for how that strong man would deal with me, or why he would block my way, I wouldn't think about it. It was even more terrifying when it was dark, because I couldn't see the surroundings. When I heard the sound of wind blowing and grass moving, I thought someone was following behind me, and my imagination was particularly rich. I didn't even dare to pay attention to the lantern I was carrying. I always broke out in a sweat when I got home.

A few months passed, and I got used to walking at night. Since I had to walk that section of road every day, imagination reduced its fear. Gradually, it became a habit. Although I inevitably had to deceive myself sometimes, another thought would immediately follow: if I really encountered any danger, let's see how he would deal with me. I remember once, a watchman was dozing off under a tree. Looking from a distance, when I recognized that it was a tired person crouching, I deliberately carried the lantern to shine clearly under the root of the tree: the watchman was awakened by the light in his sleep, thinking that something dangerous had happened, and jumped out violently. The situation was probably similar to when I was suspicious.

Now I live in the city and encounter nightlife almost every day. The night in the city is so bright. The neon lights on the tall buildings decorate the night into a beautiful world. My bedroom is close to the city center. Opening the window, I won't think of the sky covered by the black curtain of night, just like during the day. Even if I take a rickshaw home at midnight, the street lights and advertising lights on the road are always on all night. The snack shops on both sides are scraping the pots loudly, and the songs from the radio are still being sent from every building window.

Although I sometimes doze off in the rickshaw, I don't think about the night. Night has already become a part of my lifestyle, and the city is originally called a city that never sleeps. (One of the scattered notes from Rongchuang)

An Investigation into the Lives of Silkworm Farmers

— Fang Xian

Special Dispatch from This Newspaper

A single season of silkworm farming is a major undertaking for rural communities, second only to rice cultivation. Farmers depend on this season's income to cover winter expenses, various levies and taxes, and living costs before the autumn harvest. In the past, farmers even had the opportunity to prosper from silkworm rearing. However, in recent years, the Chinese silk market has been seized by foreign powers, and artificial silk has flooded the market. As a result, silk prices have plummeted, and the cocoon market has been in a perpetual slump.

Now is the time when silkworm farmers in various regions hope to reap their annual harvest. In order to understand the living conditions of silkworm farmers, I made a special trip to the countryside. Here, I would like to briefly write down my impressions. However, the central location for this investigation is Xishan in Dongting, which is known for its silk cocoon production. However, I dare say that although this is limited to a single area, the situation reflects the common plight of silkworm farmers throughout the Jiangnan region.

In this rural area of over a hundred square miles, every household must raise silkworms. In the past, when raising local varieties, they only raised silkworms twice a year—spring silkworms and summer silkworms. However, after switching to improved varieties, they now raise silkworms three or four times a year—in addition to spring and summer, there are also autumn and winter silkworms. Previously, the local varieties were mostly self-produced or purchased from Xinshi, Yuhang, or Taihu. The price of the silkworm eggs depended on the price of cocoons and the quantity of goods. However, a few years ago, several cocoon merchants established a Silkworm Improvement Guidance Center. The Silkworm Improvement Guidance Center distributed silkworm eggs to various rural households in its name. The price of the eggs was not collected at the time but was deducted at an 80% discount when the cocoons were sold. However, they have recently changed their policy, and the sellers are now a few influential individuals. Villagers who want to raise improved varieties must first register with them the year before and pay the full price of the eggs (four dimes per sheet) in advance. The following year, they can collect the eggs upon presentation of their registration certificate after Qingming. However, the egg cards are not uniform, and the quality varies depending on the relationship between the farmer and the egg sellers, and their "luck." However, it is said that this year, many people who paid for the eggs did not receive them. However, the villagers, intimidated by the power of these individuals, only dare to be angry but not speak out.

In terms of rearing, there are two types: self-rearing and communal rearing. Self-rearing involves taking the eggs and hatching them yourself. Communal rearing is where the egg sellers hatch the eggs and raise them until the first molt, when the farmers take them back. However, in addition to supplying mulberry leaves yourself, communal rearing also requires paying the egg sellers a rearing fee of six to eight dimes per sheet—elsewhere, the fee is three dimes per sheet, but here it is six to eight dimes.

Taking this year's situation as an example, each sheet of improved eggs can yield fifteen catties of silkworms at the peak of their first molt, requiring over three hundred catties of leaves. However, even with a full harvest, each sheet of eggs produces only about thirty catties of cocoons. At the current market price of thirty yuan per picul (market catty)!—the local improved varieties are thirty yuan per hundred market catties this year, while the local varieties are twenty yuan—the income is only nine yuan. Setting aside the fact that the silkworms eat their own mulberry leaves, those who buy leaves to feed their silkworms are losing money. This is because the price of green mulberry leaves this year is at least two yuan per hundred catties at the lowest, and at the highest, it is more than three yuan per hundred catties, and there is nowhere to buy them. Each sheet of eggs requires eight or nine yuan just for mulberry leaves, not including the miscellaneous expenses such as rice straw, charcoal, paper, oil, and labor.

It is said that there are three reasons for the high price of leaves this year. First, because silkworm farmers have lost money from silkworm rearing in recent years, some have cut down their mulberry trees and planted fruit trees instead, resulting in a year-on-year decrease in mulberry leaves. Second, the weather and climate this year are abnormal, and the yield of mulberry leaves is not good. Third, the price of autumn and winter cocoons last year suddenly rose to fifty-some yuan, so silkworm farmers generally believed that the price of cocoons this year would not be too low. As a result, those who raised one or two sheets in previous years increased to three or four sheets this year, and some even raised more than ten sheets. As a result, with everyone raising more, there is a natural shortage of mulberry leaves. Considering the above three reasons, the high price of leaves is a logical outcome.

The cocoon market here has always been controlled by a few local despots. Although there are more than ten cocoon firms, their organization and actions seem to be uniformly coordinated. Even though silkworm farmers know that they are being exploited, what can they do to escape their grasp?

Speaking of the manipulation of the cocoon market, many people have told me about the behind-the-scenes situation in the cocoon firms. They say that the cocoon firms originally took a 10% commission for collecting cocoons. However, in order to please the cocoon merchants and increase their commissions, they exploit the silkworm farmers as much as possible. In the past, the cocoon firms used "large" scales (eighteen taels in Suzhou, sixteen taels in Wuxi, and thirty-two taels in Huzhou, while here it was a twenty-tael scale). Since the implementation of the new measurement system, although the market scale has been changed here, the cocoon farmers weigh a full picul (thirty catties) and take it to the cocoon firm, but they are always shorted at least one to one and a half catties when the cocoon firm weighs it. Moreover, when the cocoons are poured from the cocoon farmer's basket into their large bamboo basket, and after they handle them, some become moldy and shriveled. At that time, they do not calculate according to the market price and weight but simply shout out a number: eight yuan, eight and a half yuan. The silkworm farmers call out "Sir" hundreds of times and plead for a long time. Sometimes, they might be lucky enough to get an extra two or three dimes, but sometimes they are ignored or told impatiently:

"Go! Go! If you don't sell, take it back!" If the cocoon farmer really takes it back, wanting to take it to another firm to sell for a little more, then not only will they not sell it for more, but they might even get a heavier blow, because the firms are all in cahoots. Their treatment will certainly not be different. It is pitiful to hope to sell for a few more cents, because the cocoons have already been handled once and the "color" of the sale has already been set, and it is almost more pitiful than begging.

However, this situation cannot be generalized. The weighers hired by those firms are all local ruffians and scoundrels. Naturally, they have no real experience, and there are bound to be endless abuses. Although the cocoon firm authorities and cocoon merchants know this, they have no choice because they are "local figures." Needless to say, the ones who suffer are still the silkworm farmers!

The disparity in cocoon prices is clearly unfair. This year, the purchase price of improved varieties in Suzhou is more than thirty yuan per hundred market catties, with the highest in Wuxi being forty yuan, and other places being thirty to forty yuan. However, here it has always been only about thirty yuan.—This year's cocoon production is about ten thousand piculs.

The silkworm farmers' year-long hopes, and more than a month of hard work with reduced food and sleep, are exchanged for a long string of disappointments! Those who harvest cocoons also lose money, and those who do not harvest cocoons end up with more debt. But the empty debts, levies, taxes, and living expenses... these are all heavily weighing on their hearts!

A Small Scene of Life

— Shi Feng

Almost every day is like this, I live in contradiction. I need rest, but I cannot rest. Every day, I always finish the things that need to be done within my duties before coming home. After coming home, I go to sleep. Seeing that my body cannot hold up, I do not have the courage to run out of the crowd and rest for a while. I look in the mirror: "Maintain your health!" sincere advice. However, the words of the first person rang in my ears again: "We are not celebrities, we have no time to rest, use all our energy to create a cultural rest park, then everyone can fully rest." Therefore, I am constantly working. I hold on to the present with one hand, and push him with the other, so that he can enter a new realm.

"Sir, your rent is overdue..."

"Sir, if you don't pay your meal money, we won't include it..."

I looked at those faces without any affection, and felt indescribably uncomfortable. I had no choice but to push it off, telling them a date. Although I knew very well that there was no way to do it that day either. Later, I had to come home very late, walk up the stairs lightly, and open my door gently. I went out again early the next morning. But when I came back on the third day, unexpectedly they were not asleep. As soon as they saw me walking up the stairs, their faces were so gloomy, a dark shadow covered their souls. I couldn't say a word to this situation: What to do? Keep owing it? No, I can't do that!—

"Boss, I know this is not right, but... the money from home will be sent in the next two days, I will definitely pay you back..."

Today is another day. However, I have no money to give him. Friends are so poor that they don't have any spare money. The family still hopes that I can send two back, where is the money to send? I have no choice but to come back late every night, like an abandoned cigarette butt burning alone. I still want to use this little flame to light up the torch of life and walk towards that "cultural garden."

But the iron face of life in front of me makes my timid heart tremble a little...

Promotion

— Shu Mang

The dim light shines in the dark room of the office. There is no wind, no sunlight, everything is so terribly gloomy. Whether it is the bright day or the dim night, the gray-black table is always covered with that ray of withered yellow light with a tinge of green from the desk lamp, dim, weak, and gloomy, like a ghost fire that has lost its vitality. And those people, pale, thin, each face carrying the same misery, boredom, and exhaustion, silently burying all their youth, reverie, and joy of life there. In the morning, the sun shines vigorously and brightly on the street, and people wake up from the nightmare of the night. As you suddenly walk through the piercing sunlight, you docilely drill into that dark basement-like place. There, paper books and ledgers are piled up heavily, the dim yellow and weak sunlight pitifully pouring in from the narrow windows, dust floating densely inside, looking like a stream of water. So, people pick up their pens, writing, calculating, hoping, the laughter of youth has passed, the enthusiasm of adulthood has also passed, what remains is a withered figure, and paired with this figure is a pair of withered, myopic, terrifying, empty, nothing-left eyes.

——That is career, that is work, that is life, that is... ah!

Wearing the fatigue, depression, and vagueness of a thirty-year-old, Hui Sheng angrily put down the "oolong tea" that had become hot in the tight grip of his fingers, raised his head, and felt a burst of dizziness. Some vague, green, yellow... miscellaneous auras floated in front of him, and his heart was so uncomfortable that he felt like vomiting. Then, a breath rushed up, and he coughed repeatedly. The sound floated shrilly in the air, unpleasantly ringing. The fat, Western-dressed second-class clerk sitting opposite Hui Sheng glanced at him disgustedly, then put on a sympathetic, pitying look, frowned, and began to speak didactically:

"I think, Secretary Wang, you should take a long vacation and go back to rest? Such hard work is not good for you. You are still young, your health is important, as for... as for..." He wanted to say "as for money... as for life..." but he felt that these were too difficult to say, too difficult to make sound good, so he unnaturally stopped his words, and looked at the pale and terrible face sitting opposite him with a sincere look, as if eager to get an answer.

"It doesn't matter, it doesn't matter... it's just a cold, a bit of a cough... Yes, things are hard, things... but the higher-ups always know, it's always good to have someone promote you. If you get someone's promotion, then everything will be a little better..."

The man cast a contemptuous glance, then laughed loudly "Hahahaha". The laughter painfully stung in Hui Sheng's ears, he felt a little! Four years, at the age of twenty-seven, he finally managed to get such a position through friends and relatives: as a copyist, twenty-five yuan salary, food and accommodation at his own expense... Under such conditions, he still had to support a family of four. However, "people have to rely on others to promote them!" Four years, still twenty-five yuan, still "Secretary Wang", still respectfully saluting the bald-headed section chief. These, all exist vaguely. "Promotion" is important, like Little Li, who came two years later than him, didn't he get promoted to second-class clerk last month? However... people say that Little Li has a sister who is on good terms with the section chief... in that way, people get "promoted". And myself...

Hope is as dark and vague as the light on the table, and the body is getting worse day by day, how long will this day last?…

The door was pushed open, revealing a bald head and a pair of black-rimmed glasses, so, habitually, "Secretary Wang" stood up respectfully again…
